==PAGE:3==
II Abstract
iii
II
Abstract
The feasibility of High Temperature Superconductor (HTS) Coated Conductors (CCs)
as a basis for bolometric detectors were investigated and found possible. Detectors were
produced from CCs with two diﬀerent substrate thicknesses to investigate this eﬀect on
the response of the bolometers. A thermal bridge setup was designed in order to mea-
sure the eﬀect of the substrate thickness compared to the otherwise dominating eﬀect of
the thermal contact resistance between the substrate and coldhead. Using this setup the
thicker substrate was found to give a better response of the bolometer.
The noise in the data is dominated by excess noise in the measurement setup. The Noise
Equivalent Power (NEP) for the detectors was found to be in the range from 3 · 10−7 to
8·10−6 W · Hz−1/2. The corresponding detectivity, D∗, of the devices were found to be in
the range from 105 to 5 · 105 cm ·
√
Hz · W−1. This is an order of magnitude lower than
other HTS bolometers in the literature.
With an impulse response, Di, of 8 · 10−5 cm · J−1, the produced bolometers are found
comparable to other bolometers in the literature. By reducing the excess noise in the
measurement setup, an improvement of 2 orders of magnitude is predicted for the NEP,
D∗and Di.
The predictability of a simple RC model was investigated. The model was found to pro-
vide a good prediction of the measured response, though at high frequencies frequency
dependent eﬀects start to dominate and a discrepancy is seen between the model pre-
diction and the measurements. A model to simulate the thermal behavior of the system
was proposed. However, it was not possible to ﬁnish the simulation model within the
relatively short time frame of the present project.
The coated conductors was found to be a feasible template for bolometer detectors and the
eﬀect of the substrate thickness on the responsivity of the bolometer was documented.
The the simple RC model was found to give a good prediction of the response of the
bolometers.

1 Introduction
1
1
Introduction
Humans have looked at the heaven for thousands of years wondering about the origin of
the heavenly bodies seen in the sky. Dating all the way back to the myths and legends of
ancient times, astronomy has a great heritage of explaining the heavenly phenomena [1].
The better understanding of the heaven has been driven by the technological development
allowing to overcome limitations of the naked eye using telescopes, such as the Very Large
Telescope and Extremely Large Telescope [2, 3, 4].
Furthermore, the opaqueness of the atmosphere at most wavelengths [5] was overcome
when satellites became a platform for astronomical observations after the space race in
the 1960s. No longer limited by the atmosphere the possible observations were suddenly
limited by the instruments on board the spacecrafts that were sent beyond the Earth’s
atmosphere. A continuous development of better instruments result in observations of
still fainter sources, such as galaxies and stars that are millions of lightyears away.
The detector considered in the present work is a Transition Edge Sensor (TES). We
will start with a short introduction to the space applications before we introduce the
detector. After the theoretical background for the bolometer is established in Section 3
the experimental work is presented in Section 4.
Finally, the ﬁndings are presented,
discussed and concluded in Sections 5 to 7.
1.1
Space application
The infrared (IR) region of the electromagnetic spectrum, with wavelengths ranging from
1 µm to 1 mm, covers a wide range of astronomical information. In this region we ﬁnd
radiation from interstellar dust, planets and the Cosmic microwave background (CMB) [6].
Observing these provide insight to the structure and formation of planets, stars, galaxies
and intergalactic clusters [6].
Figure 1.1 illustrates the diﬀerent astronomical sources
represented as Planck black body curves. Bolometers are typically used to measure the
emission of cold intergalactic dust, heated by starlight, as well as studying the mechanisms
and rates of star formation [6]. Narrow band (semicondutor) detectors such as the Si-
CCD detector give a good sensitivity. However, these detectors have a cut-oﬀfrequency
limited by the bandgap, where the bolometer respond to a wider range of wavelengths
[7, 8]. A bolometer can thus provide a sensitive detector at wavelengths from micrometers
to x-rays [8] by the use of a frequency independent antenna as described by Kreisler and
Gaugue [9].
The amount of equipment that can be sent on a space mission is highly limited by the
rocket used.
For example a small and large rocket can carry 22.8 ton and 54.4 ton
respectively to Low Earth Orbit (LEO), i.e. 160-2000 km altitude, for the price of 2000-
3000 $/kg [10] (see also Figure B.1). For missions going to deep space, i.e. escaping
the gravitational pull of the Earth, this is reduced to 4 and 13.6 ton with a price of
approximately 7000 to 15000 $/kg. Thus missions going beyond Earth, e.g. studying
outer planets, are highly resource constrained and cooling options are limited passive or
single stage coolers with a lower limit of 60 K [11]. The sensor proposed in this work
operates at 90 K, which is well above this limit. Thus, the spacecraft can be designed
without use of multistage cooling and coolants like Helium. The freed up volume/weight
can thus be used for extra scientiﬁc instruments. Furthermore, the lifetime of a mission
can be extended when it is not dependent on coolants like Helium, which is diﬃcult to

Herschel that is being decommissioned now that it has run out of Helium [12].
A detector that requires less cooling while still providing a good sensitivity is the optimal
choice for observations in space.
In particular good sensors are wanted in the 25 µm
to 1 mm, where semiconductor detectors are limited by the bandgap [11, 7, 8].
The
bolometetric detector works well in this range. The next section will take a closer look at
this type of detector.
1.2
The Bolometer
This section will introduce the basics of the bolometer. A more elaborate treatment can
be found in Section 3.3.
A schematic of a bolometer is shown in Figure 1.2(a).
The bolometer consists of a
radiation absorber and a thermometer coupled to a heat sink. The incoming radiation
is converted to heat in the absorber and the resulting temperature can be read using
the thermometer, typically an electrical resistance Re [ Ω]. In the case of constant bias
current I, a small signal-to-signal voltage output ∆U = I dRe
dT · ∆T [ V] is proportional to
the slope of the resistance versus temperature curve of the thermometer close to T0. The

There are many diﬀerent kinds of bolometric sensors. One such is the Large Apex Bolome-

Figure 1.2(b).
In the next section we will look more on the diﬀerent sensors and in

==PAGE:10==
1.3
Transition Edge Sensor (TES)
4
1.3
Transition Edge Sensor (TES)
The response of the bolometric sensors is based on a variation of the temperature in
the sensor. For this reason many diﬀerent materials can be used resulting in a variety
of sensors utilizing diﬀerent physical phenomena to create an electrical signal from the
thermal variation in the material. As a result, there exist sensors operating in diﬀerent
temperature ranges from room temperature down to a few mK. Uncooled (∼300 K)
and highly cooled (∼4 K and below) detectors are readily available in the Infrared (IR)
spectrum [11]. However, moderately cooled detectors (∼70 K) are available as quantum
detectors up to 5 µm (InSb) 15-20 µm (HgCdTe), but in general not for the Far-infrared
(FIR) spectrum from about 25 µm to 1 mm [11]. The high Tc superconductor bolometer
being the major exception.
The sensor considered in the present project is a TES, which utilize the the large change in
electrical resistance of a superconductor when it changes from the superconducting state
to the normal conducting state, as described in Section 3.1.1. This transition happens at a
temperature called the critical temperature Tc, which varies for diﬀerent superconductors.
YBa2Cu3O7−x (YBCO) exhibits a reﬂectivity above 97 % for wavelengths λ > 20 µm as
reported by Zhang and Flik [15]. Two currently adopted solutions to couple the incident
radiation to the thermally sensitive superconduter are [9]: Absorption layer coupling and
antenna coupling. However, ﬁnding the optimum absorption layer or antenna is outside
the scope of this project. Instead the YBCO is irradiated with λ ≪20 µm to ensure good
absorption.
Figure 1.3 compares diﬀerent superconducting bolometers at both high Tc (with a Tc
around 90 K) and low Tc (with Tc at 0.1 K, 3.7 K and 7.7 K) with room tempera-
ture bolometers in terms of the speciﬁc detectivity versus response time for diﬀerent an-
tenna coupled superconducting bolometers. The best detectors have NEP values close
Figure 1.3: Comparison of diﬀerent antenna-coupled bolometers, as presented in the text.
Figure reproduced from [9]. Data is seen in Table C.1.
to the phonon noise limited prediction of 2 · 10−12
W
√
Hz [9].

D∗

coupled superconducting bolometers [13].
==PAGE:11==
1.4
Motivation
5
It is seen that the High Temperature Superconductor (HTS) detectors provide a better
detectivity than the room temperature devices. However, the Low Temperature Super-
conductor (LTS) detectors are seen to provide a better detectivity, which is due to the
lower noise present at the lower temperature [11]. As mentioned previously the disad-
vantage with the lower temperature is the requirement for more complex cooling systems
to reach the < 10 K for the detectors in the ﬁgure. It is thus a trade-oﬀbetween the
sensitivity of the detector and complexity and cost of the system to operate the device.
The detector considered in the present work is a HTS operating at the higher temperature
of 90 K. In the next section we will take a closer look at the motivation for looking at
this particular sensor.
1.4
Motivation
The HTS TES provides a better detectivity than the corresponding room temperature
devices, while avoiding the need for the very complex and expensive Helium cooling
systems required for LTS detectors. With an operational temperature above the boiling
point of 77 K for the rather inexpensive coolant Liquid Nitrogen (LN2) the HTS detectors
can provide an improved sensitivity in applications where the complex cooling systems
cannot be used, such as for deep space mission.
Besides the operating temperature there exist other ways to improve the detectivity of
the sensor. The response of the bolometer can be improved by decreasing the thermal
link between the heat sink and the sensing element.
One such approach is to isolate
the superconducting ﬁlm from the substrate by inserting a multilayer stack between the
two. Thus achieving the weak thermal link by acoustical phonon mismatch between the
lattice structures [13]. The Coated Conductors (CCs) considered in this project exhibit
such a layered structure, as described in Section 4.1.1. Another advantage of the layered
structure is the added rigidity and thus an improved mechanical strength of the device
[13, 16].
The response of the device can also be improved by increasing the dRe
ddT of the thermometer.
In the CC thin ﬁlm superconducting layer is used, the smaller cross sectional area of
the then ﬁlm greatly increases the resistance thus improving the response of the sensor.
Furthermore, the small size of the thin ﬁlm superconducting layer used in the CC result in
a small heat capacity of the thermally active region as well as smaller thermal conductance
from the ﬁlm into the substrate. As a consequence, a low noise, fast response and high
voltage responsivity is achieved [16]. In fact a phonon noise-limited electrical NEP of
4.5·10−12
W
√
Hz at 10 kHz modulation frequency and a responsivity of 478 V
W for an YBCO
thin ﬁlm with a bias of 550µ A is reported by Richards [16].
The HTS TES operates in a temperature range where inexpensive coolants are available.
Thus providing a good detection in applications where the complexity of the system is
limited. The coated conductors are commercially available and exhibit a layered structure
that can be used to improve the response of the bolometer. Several groups have reported
HTS detectors on single crystalline substrates [17, 16, 18]. However, the present project
will investigate the feasibility of the CCs for bolometric detectors. To the best knowledge
of our knowledge this is the ﬁrst time a bolometric detectors based on commercially
available coated conductors are reported.
We will now present the hypotheses to be
tested on the CC in the present work.
==PAGE:12==
2 Research hypotheses
6
2
Research hypotheses
The high temperature superconductor used in CCs and their commercial availability opens
the possibility of producing cheaper bolometric detectors.
In the present work three
research hypotheses have been identiﬁed as important aspects to be investigated for the
possibly future use of CC based bolometric detectors.
1. A coated conductor is a feasible template for making an operational bolo-
metric sensor compared to currently available sensors.
The feasibility of the produced bolometric detectors is investigated by comparing the
detectors with currently available single crystalline detectors.
Three ﬁgures of merit
commonly used on comparing the detectors will be used: The Noise Equivalent Power,
detectivity and the impulse response.
2. The thickness of the substrate can be documented to have an eﬀect on the
responsivity.
The thickness of the substrate changes the heat transfer through material. It will be
investigated if this eﬀect can be measured beside the other thermal eﬀects in the system
that might be dominant, such as thermal contact resistance.
3. The responsivity can be predicted using a simple RC model.
Diﬀerent models are available to predict the response of bolometric detectors. To the
best of our knowledge the modeling of a CC based bolometer is currently not available.
It is therefore important to investigate the predictability of a simple RC model on the
response of a CC based detector or whether a new model for CC based HTS bolometers
is needed.
3
Theory
In the present project a thin ﬁlm of the high temperature superconducting material YBCO
will be used as both absorption layer and resistor in the device. The necessary under-
standing of superconductivity is introduced in Section 3.1.3. The response of a bolometer
is based on heat transfer between diﬀerent materials. Thus Section 3.2 will introduce the
thermal theory needed to consider the bolometric theory in Section 3.3. Finally the simple
RC model for a bolometric detector is presented in Section 3.3.1. Finally, a simulation
approach to represent the bolometric system is presented in Section 3.4.
3.1
Superconductivity
Superconductivity was discovered in 1911 when a sudden drop in resistance of mercury was
reported by Onnes [19]. Soon after, tin and lead were added to the list of superconducting
materials [20]. The change in resistance at the critical temperature Tc is presented in
Section 3.1.1. The critical current Ic, is another limitation on the superconducting state
and is described in Section 3.1.3.
Besides the use for MR scanners in hospitals, superconductivity was primarily a phe-
nomenon in the laboratory until a new class of superconductors, called high-Tc cuprates,
were discovered by Bednorz and M¨uller [21] in 1986.
Only a year later, in 1987, the
==PAGE:13==
3.1
Superconductivity
7
cuprate YBCO was synthesized by Wu et al. [22], see Section 4.1.1 for more details.
Even though YBCO was synthesized in 1987 [22] the deeper understanding of the high
temperature superconductivity is still missing. There are many theories [23], but not
a general one that most scientist agree on, as is the case with the BCS theory for low
temperature superconductivity.
Two of the most fundamental properties of superconductors are the changes in resistance
and magnetic susceptibility.
3.1.1
Resistivity
The ﬁrst property we will look at is the drop in resistance at the critical temperature
Tc. Figure 3.1(a) sketches the electric resistivity, ρe, as a function of temperature. The
resistivity of the superconductor exhibit the same behavior as a normal conductor above
the critical temperature. However, at Tc the resistivity of the superconductor suddenly
drops to zero.
In reality zero resistance is unmeasurable, as there will be limitations on the zero output of
the equipment used or one would simply be measuring the resistance in the wires used in
the setup. The same is the case for conductivity σ = ρ−1
e
which would be inﬁnitely large.
However, a drop in resistivity (or increase in conductivity) by many orders of magnitude
is possible and thus it is possible to arrive at a reasonable conclusion that ρe →0 or
σ →∞using real measurements [24].
By measuring the resistivity of the superconductor as a function of temperature, the
critical temperature Tc can be derived from the corresponding R-T curve as sketched in
Figure 3.1(a).
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.1: Sketches of the two fundamental properties of superconductors. Figures repro-
duced from [24]. (a) The resistivity as a function of temperature for a normal conductor
and a superconductor respectively. The sudden drop of resistivity to ρe = 0 is seen at the
critical temperature Tc compared to a normal conductor where it decreases linearly. (b)
The magnetic susceptibility χ as a function of temperature shows the drop from a small
positive value (paramagnetic) above Tc to χ = −1, i.e. perfect diamagnet, below Tc.
==PAGE:14==
3.2
Heat transfer
8
3.1.2
Susceptibility
The second fundamental property is the change of the magnetic susceptibility χ from a
small positive value to the value of a perfect diamagnet, χ = −1 below Tc, as indicated
in Figure 3.1(b).
Meissner and Ochsenfeld [25] showed that a magnetic ﬁeld applied
above Tc was expelled from the interior of the superconductor when cooled below Tc, i.e.
|⃗B| = 0 T inside the superconductor. Writing the magnetic ﬁeld in terms of the auxiliary
ﬁeld ⃗H
 A
m

and the magnetization ⃗M
 A
m

gives [26]:
⃗B = µ0 ·

⃗H + ⃗M

= 0,
where µ0 = 4π · 10−7 T· m
A
is the vacuum permeability. Thus giving the magnetization
⃗M = −⃗H and the perfect diamagnetic susceptibility:
χ =
⃗M
⃗H
= −1.
The critical temperature Tc can also be estimated from a susceptibility measurement
producing a curve as shown in Figure 3.1(b).
When the temperature is above Tc, i.e. in the normal conducting state, the applied ﬁeld
can pass through the superconductor. Below Tc the ﬁeld is expelled. However, there is a
limit to how large a ﬁeld that can be expelled called the critical ﬁeld Hc. The diﬀerent
critical parameters are presented in the following section.
3.1.3
Critical parameters
The superconductor has several critical parameters that limit when superconductivity
occurs.
The ﬁrst one is the critical temperature Tc, mentioned in Section 3.1.1.
For
T > Tc the superconductor is in the normal conducting state. The second parameter is
the critical ﬁeld Hc (or Hc1 and Hc2 in the case of type II superconductors) discussed in
Section 3.1.2 and Appendix D.1.3.
The third critical parameter is the critical current density Jc. Several ways to measure
and in practice deﬁne the critical current [27] exists. One such is by the use of transport
current (see Appendix D.1.5).
In the TES the transition at the critical temperature is utilized. It is therefore important
that the detector is biased with currents well below the critical current Ic and not exposed
to large magnetic ﬁelds. Thus the bolometer should ideally be close to the Tc axis on
Figure 3.2.
In the TES bolometer the change in temperature is used to generate a change in resistance
an thus a measurable response. In the next section we will consider the heat transfer
processes behind the temperture changes of the bolometer.
3.2
Heat transfer
An essential part of a bolometer is the absorption and transport of heat. This section will
introduce the theory of heat transfer. The diﬀerent modes of heat transfer is described

3.2.1
Modes of heat transfer
A temperature diﬀerence is required for a heat transfer process to take place. The heat
transfer will always be from the medium with the highest temperature to the one with
the lowest. The three modes of heat transfer are [29, 28]:
• Conduction: The heat transfer through a given material or two materials in con-
tact.
• Convection: The energy transfer between a gas or ﬂuid adjacent to a surface,
through both conduction and ﬂuid motion.
• Radiation: The transfer of energy through electromagnetic radiation. Radiation
heat transfer does not require an intervening medium.
The system analyzed in this work involves only solids in vacuum, thus the convective heat
transfer can be disregarded. However, heat transfer through conduction and radiation are
a key parts of the workings of a bolometer and will thus be introduced in more detail
below.
==PAGE:16==

Heat transfer

3.2.4
Contact resistance
A seemingly smooth surface of a solid will on a microscopic scale have a roughness that
will result in a less than perfect thermal contact between two solids pressed together.
Figure 3.4(a) shows a sketch of such a contact. The heat transfer between the solids
follows two paths, as indicated on the ﬁgure. The conduction between the solid-solid
interface is very eﬀective, while the heat transfer through the interstices is relatively
poor, as it is mediated through radiation or convection in the substance (if any) in the
interstices [28].

3.3
Bolometer
The bolometer was invented around 1880 by Samuel Pierpont Langley and has since
become an important part of research in black body radiation [31].
In the simplest
form the bolometer essentially consists of three parts[32]: Firstly, an absorber where the
incident energy is converted into a temperature increase. Secondly, a sensor that reads
out the temperature increase. Finally a link between the detector and the heat sink that
ensures a thermal coupling, through which the absorbed heat can be dumped from the
detector. Figure 3.5 shows a sketch of a simple bolometric detector.

3.3

3.3.1
RC model
The input power absorbed in the bolometer often has a steady part and a time varying
part given by [33]:
Pin = P0 + P1 · ei·ωs·t,
(3.12)
where ωs [ Hz] is the frequency, P0 , P1 [ W] are the amplitudes of the steady and time-
varying part respectively. Consequently the temperature has a similar variation [33]:
TB = T0 + T1 · ei·ωs·t,
where ωs [ Hz] is the frequency, T0 , T1 [ K] are the amplitudes of the steady and time
varying part respectively. The bolometer contains a resistive thermometer biased with
either a constant voltage or a constant current, as discussed below.
Electrothermal feedback
When the incoming light rays are absorbed by the detector,
the temperature of the absorber, i.e. the superconducting layer, is raised. This in turn
leads to a change in resistance. However, if the raise in temperature is enough to push
the superconductor into the normal conducting state, as mentioned in Section 3.1.1, the
change in resistance will result in a change in the Joule heating of the sensor:
PJoule = I2 · Re = U2
Re
,
(3.13)
where U [ V] is the electrical potential, I [A] is the current, Re = ρe·l
A [ Ω] is the electrical
resistanceof a slap with corssectional area A and length l. PJoule [ W] is the power dissi-
pated in the sensor. If the sensor is current biased, i.e. I = U
R = constant, the increased
resistance leads to a rise in the Joule heating and a resulting higher temperature. As a
result a positive feedback is created and thermal runaway heating up the superconductor
can happen. However, when the resistance decreases as a result of the bolometer relaxing
back to the initial sink temperature, the dissipated power will decrease resulting in a
faster cool down.
If the sensor is voltage biased, i.e. U = R · I = constant, the rise in resistance results in
a lower power dissipated in the sensor and thus a lower resistance further decreasing the
power dissipated in the sensor. The voltage biased sensor thus has a negative feedback
loop, that serves to stabilize the sensor-temperature.
Nevertheless, when the voltage
biased bolometer is relaxing back to the initial sink temperature the opposite eﬀect occurs.
In this case the Joule heating will actually increase, thus increasing the time it will take
the bolometer to relax back to the sink temperature.
For example, if the sensor is biased with 1 V or 0.1 A and the resistance changes with
10 Ω:
Current −biased :
(0.1 A)2 · 10 Ω= 0.1 W > (0.1 A)2 · 20 Ω= 0.2 W,
Voltage −biased :
(1 V)2
10 Ω= 0.1 W > (1 V)2
20 Ω= 0.05 W.
With constant voltage a change in resistance leads to a change in current which is inversely
proportional to the increase in resistance, thus a voltage biased bolometer requires a very
==PAGE:21==
3.3
Bolometer
15
sensitive ammeter in order to detect the small ﬂuctuations in the current. An example
of such device is a superconducting quantum interference device (SQUID) [36], though
a SQUID also requires cooling below Tc which means that the SQUID possibly needs
separate cooling below the coldhead temperature, usually held at Tc of the superconductor
in the bolometer. On the other hand, the current biased bolometer results in a change
in voltage proportional to the increase in resistance and sensitive voltmeters are fairly
common [33]. Thus for most applications a current biased bolometer is the most feasible
solution.

3.3.2
Noise Equivalent Power (NEP)
The sensitivity of direct detectors are commonly expressed in terms of Noise Equivalent
Power (NEP)
h
W
√
Hz
i
, deﬁned as the input signal power required to deliver a signal-to-noise
ratio equal to unity in a 1 Hz bandwidth [13]. The NEP for a high Tc superconducting
bolometer consists of the contributions from [8]:
• NEPphot: Photon noise in the incident radiation from a background with the tem-
perature TB
• NEPphon: Thermal noise caused by the exchange of phonons between the bolometer
and the heat sink
• NEPJ: Johnson noise in the thermometer resistance of the superconductor
• NEPf: Excess low frequency noise in the ﬁlm
• NEPamp: Noise associated with an ampliﬁer having the noise temperature TN.

3.4
Simulation
The heat transfer for a simple system, as shown in Figure 3.5, can be treated fairly well. As
the complexity of the system increases with several layers of diﬀerent materials in contact
with each other, the continuously varying temperatures as a function of location and time
make calculating the temperature ﬁelds in detail very cumbersome. Dividing the system
in discrete regions in which temperature gradients can be neglected, termed isothermal
nodes. These regions have a characteristic temperature, heat capacity, heat diﬀusion as
well as conductive and radiative interfaces with surrounding nodes. A discretized thermal
circuit is sketched in Figure 4.2.

4
Experimental part
In this section we will cover the experimental work performed in the present project. We
will start with the fabrication of the bolometers from the CCs in Section 4.1. We will then
cover the assembled measurement setup in Section 4.2 and the developed data acquisition
software in Section 4.3.
==PAGE:25==
4.1
Samples

4.1
Samples
The fabrication of the bolometer detectors used in this work are done using Coated
Conductors (CCs) from SuperPower Inc. The structure of the CCs and the process used
create the bolometer sensor is described in the following.
4.1.1
Coated Conductors and YBCO-based superconductors
Many of the high-Tc compounds have a quasi-two-dimensional network of CuO2, i.e.
cuprate perovskites. They are highly anisotropic and can behave as semiconductors along
the c-axis while possessing metal-like conductivity in the direction parallel to the CuO2
planes [24]. The structure of YBCO is shown in Figure 4.1, where the anisotropy is clear
from the stacking of the diﬀerent layers along the c-axis.

C-axis
Figure 4.1: The structure of Y Ba2Cu3O6. The layers continue in the plane orthogonal to
the c-axis, while the anisotropy is clearly seen by the two diﬀerent layers along that axis.
From [24].
Very high values of Jc ≈106
A
cm2 have been observed for YBCO ﬁlms grown on single
crystalline substrates such as SrTiO3 [38]. However, single crystalline ceramic substrates
are not suitable for production of REBCO CCs with length in the kilometer range. The
main requirements for a practical substrate are [39]:
• A strong bi-axial texture.
• A lattice parameter similar to the REBCO.
• A smooth surface.
• Mechanical strength and ﬂexibility
The samples used in this work is provided by SuperPower Inc. Where a 50 or 100 µm
Hastelloy substrate is used along with ﬁve buﬀer layers to fulﬁll the above requirements
[40]. The structure of the SuperPower tapes is sketched in Figure 4.2(a). The buﬀer
==PAGE:26==
4.1
Samples
20
layers serve to ensure both an optimal lattice structure on which the REBCO can be
grown, as well as a diﬀusion barrier to avoid loss of the doped oxygen [39]. An Ag layer
is deposited for chemical stability, diﬀusion barrier and providing a localized conduction
in case a quench occurs. A Cu layer is used for further stability and quench protection.
However, the CCs used in this work are without the Cu layer.

4.1.2
Silver etch
The silver coating on the protecting the YBCO layer from exposure to air [42] unfortu-
nately also has the side eﬀect that it strongly reﬂects the incoming radiation. For that
reason, it is necessary to remove the silver coating. In [43] it is suggested to use a solution
of 25 % NH3OH, 25 % H2O2 and 50 % H2O. This method was tested and was found very
eﬀective as the 50 µm silver coating was etched away in this solution in only 12 seconds.
The etched sample is shown in the inset on Figure 4.3(a), where it is seen that the silver
coating has been removed where the CC tape was not protected by a layer of Kapton
tape.
(a)
(b)
Figure 4.3: The ﬁrst etched sample of the superconducting tape. (a) a SEM image of the
bare superconductor shown in the inset. The black in the middle of the inset is the free
YBCO superconducting layer and the two silver colored ends of the sample are the silver
coating, that has not been etched away, as it was protected by Kapton Tape. The area
shown in the image is used for the Energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) analysis
shown in (b).
A diluted version of the above etch solution consisting of 10 % NH3OH, 10 % H2O2 and
==PAGE:27==
4.1
Samples
21
80 % H2O was tried. As a result the slower etch rate removed the the silver coating in 4
minutes. This provides a better control of the etching. Thus the diluted version is used
in the further processing.
Inspection of the sample after the etch showed a few silver spots remaining on the sample.
Bubbles formed by H2O2 on the surface of the superconductor covered the silver and thus
hindered a further etch underneath. For this reason the sample was etched further for 4
minutes in a fresh solution, where the sample was lifted out of the etch solution and put
back several times to remove the bubbles and allow for etching of the remaining silver. A
similar approach is used in all the following wet processing steps by stirring the solutions.
To investigate if the silver was indeed removed, on a microscopic scale the sample was
put in a Scanning Electron Microscope (SEM) for further investigation. The SEM image
is shown in Figure 4.3(a). The ﬁgure shows an area of the bare superconductor that
is used for Energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) analysis seen on Figure 4.3(b).
The analysis shows that the peaks of silver are not identiﬁable in the given area, which
indicates that the silver has been etched away. The silver spots visible on the surface
by the naked eye therefore must be because of the bubbles forming on the surface as
mentioned previously. It is therefore important that the solutions are stirred during the
wet chemical processing. The surface seen on the SEM is quite rough and could indicate
that it is still covered with parts of the coating. However, EDS analysis of diﬀerent spots
showed that it is the YBCO surface that is rough, as can be seen in Figure F.3 on page 68
in the Appendix.

4.2
Setup
An automatic measurement system was developed for measuring the voltage response of
the bolometer for various frequencies, as well as measuring the resistance versus tempera-
ture. The setup consists of several parts described in the following sections: The cryogenic
system in Section 4.2.1, the assembled optical setup in Section 4.2.2, the hardware used
in the setup in Section 4.2.3 and the developed automatic measurement software in Sec-
tion 4.3. The susceptibility measurements were performed using the setup described in
Section 4.2.4.
4.2.1
Cryogenic system
The cryogenic system consists of a continuous ﬂow cryostat, Microstat HE2, with a tem-
perature sensor and heater connected to a temperature controller, Mecury iTC. The cool-
ing was done with liquid nitrogen using a LLT transfer tube and a VC-U ﬂow controller
all from Oxford Instruments Omicron NanoScience. A sketch of the setup is shown in
Figure 4.7. However, the cryostat was not prepared for electrical contacts to the sample
and the following modiﬁcations had to be made:
Internal wiring
The operating temperature range for the cryostat ranges from tem-
peratures below 4 K to 500 K [45]. Thus the wire used to make the connections inside
the cryostat must be able to withstand these temperature variations. The chosen wire SH
Therm 210 Grd.2 from SH Elektrodraht has a very good thermal and overload resistance
==PAGE:30==
4.2
Setup
24
(a)
(b)
(c)
Figure 4.6: One of the produced samples mounted on the coldhead of the cryostat (illustrated
in Figures 4.7 and 4.8). (a) shows the sample holder with the electrical contacts soldered
to one end and the wirebonding between the sample and the sample holder. (b) is a zoom in
showing the wirebonds. (c) shows the back of the coldﬁnger where the samples is mounted.
[46]. Though these properties also made it harder to work with the wire, as described in
Appendix E.3.1 on page 64.
Connector
The cryostat was equipped with a non-standard plug, GH10, from Positronic
industries [47], which could be used to connect to the cryostat using the temperature con-
troller cable from another Oxford Cryostat, by making a 9-pin D-SUB to BNC connector
plug. However, there were issues with the connection and a GH10 to BNC connector was
made to ensure a proper connection.
4.2.2
Optical Setup
Figure 4.8 shows a sketch of the optical setup, where a chopper, New Focus Model 3502,
is used to modulate the frequencies of the laser beam hitting the bolometer. The chopper
wheels and corresponding frequencies are shown in Table 4.3. In order to modulate at
the higher frequencies it was necessary to use a chopper wheel with 100 slots. However,
the size of the slots in the 100 slot wheel was smaller than the spot size of the laser.
Thus using a lens to focus the laser beam to a point that allowed modulation at the
higher frequencies provided by the 100 slot blade. Thus the beam had to be re-collimated
after the chopper using a second lens. Using two mirrors the beam path was extended to
provide space for the setup. Finally the beam is focused into a spot with an area covering
the sensor. The laser used in the experiment was a red 635 nm laser from Thorlabs. The
==PAGE:31==

uniform. The laser spot is focused so the intense part of the spot covers the area equal
to the width of the meander line and the length between the silver pads. The power of
the laser beam in front of the sample was measured to 2.296 mW ±0.005 mW, thus the

4.2.3
Hardware
The hardware used in the setup consists of both a part supplying the bias to the bolometer
as well as a part for the data acquisition as described in the following.
==PAGE:32==
4.2
Setup
26
Figure 4.8: The optical setup used to measure the responsivity and resistivity of the samples.
A red 635 nm laser is chopped with an optical chopper using three diﬀerent chopper wheels.
The inset in the top left corner shows the laser spot.
Bias current
As suggested in [48] it was initially sought to have a constant current
supply using a bias resistor and a battery. In this case a current limited power supply
from Thurlby and Thandar, TTi QL335TP, was tried using a 33 k Ωresistor as bias
resistor. Unfortunately this proved to be quite unstable in supplying the current. It takes
about 5 minutes from it is turned on until it reaches some sort of plateau, as seen in
Figure 4.9.
For this reason a current supply circuit was developed using a voltage regulator, an
operation ampliﬁer and a transistor, as seen in Figure 4.10 (due to replacement of some
part the ﬁrst measurements were taken with the supply seen in Figure E.4 on page 65).
The current supply circuit is powered using a triple output Agilent E3631A power supply,
with +15 V and -15 V supply operated in track mode for best stability [49]. The stability
of the current supply circuit is plotted as red in Figure 4.9 alongside the TTi supply. It
is seen that the current circuit greatly improves the stability. Not only does it reach a
stable level fast, compared to the approximately 5 minutes of the TTi supply. It also has
far less variation of the current load. The standard deviation of the improved current
supply circuit is 4.3548 · 10−8 A.
Data acquisition
The resistances, voltages and currents are measured using two Agi-
lent 34401A multimeters in the corresponding modes. The resistance measurements were
done using the four probe mode.
A Stanford Research Lock-in ampliﬁer model SR830 was used to measure the voltage re-
sponse from the samples. The chopper was phase-locked to the output from the internal
oscillator of the lock-in ampliﬁer, as this enabled software controlled frequency modula-
tion. However, when the internal oscillator is used for frequency modulation the lock-in
cannot be phase-locked to an external source. Thus any deviation from the frequency due
==PAGE:33==

to jitter in the chopper wheel will not be picked up. As a result the jitter for the chopper
wheels as indicated in Table 4.3, will result in an uncertainty of the modulation phase of
the laser.
4.2.4
Susceptibility Setup
As mentioned in Section 3.1.2 the susceptibility of the superconductor drops from a pos-
itive value to χ = −1 when crossing Tc. It is a measure of the sample’s magnetization
and ac susceptibility measurements involve a varying magnetic ﬁeld Hac and recording
the samples response using a sensing coil. Thus [50]:
χ = dM
dHac
A sketch of the measurement setup is shown in Figure E.5 on page 65 in Appendix. The
sample is placed in a sensing coil (secondary coil) while the primary coil is applying the
magnetic ﬁeld Hac. The magnetic ﬂux variation due to the magnetization of the sample
is picked up by the sensing coil. A second sensing coil is connected to the ﬁrst coil, but in
opposite direction. This ensures that the voltages induced by the ac ﬁeld or other external
sources are canceled. For an in depth treatment of ac susceptibility measurements the
reader is referred to the guide by Nikolo [50].
4.3
Automatic data acquisition software
The available measurement software was not suitable for the setup previously described.
As a result a new set of measurement software was developed using the commercially
available software Labview. The Frontpanels of the programs and corresponding Labview
block diagrams, i.e. the code, are shown in Figures H.3 to H.5 on page 77 and on page 78.

First we will introduce the processes common to all the programs. Using the simplest
software, the program to measure the stability of the current supply, these processes will
be introduced before we move to the more complex software. All of the programs are
designed to save all information from the program along with the data. We will show
the ﬂow diagram of the largest program, the one for response measurements. The ﬂow
diagrams for the other programs is found in Appendix H.1.1 on page 76.
4.3.1
Current supply stability
This program is very simple and runs a loop where measurements are taken continuously
and saved along with the time from the beginning of the measurements. The initialize
and close routine which is used in all the programs are described below. The ﬂow diagram
for the program is found in Figure H.1 on page 76 in Appendix.
Initialize
The ﬁrst thing the programs do are to prepare all the data ﬁles and save
all settings from the program to a settings ﬁle to make it accessible, if needed, when
processing the data. Furthermore, connections are established to all equipments, which
is then conﬁgure according to the measurements to be taken.
It also incorporates a
conﬁrmation if the user is about to overwrite a previous data set.
Close
Similarly, the closing routine close connections to all the equipment, ensuring
that all power supplies are turned oﬀand the equipment is shut down. The data ﬁles are
closed and the remaining information is save to the info ﬁle/settings ﬁle, such as if the
==PAGE:35==
4.3
Automatic data acquisition software
29
program completed or was manually terminated. Also information regarding erros or if
limits have been reached are saved in this process
4.3.2
Resistance
The second program developed is for measuring the resistance of the superconductor
as a function of temperature. This is done in two steps, ﬁrst a coarse measurement is
done over a wide temperature range with longer temperature steps, such as 1 K between
each data point.
Using the coarse measurements the program can estimate Tc and a
measurement series with a ﬁner temperature step can be performed in a given range
around the estimated Tc. The ﬂow diagram is seen in Figure H.2 on page 77 in Appendix.
Both the coarse and ﬁne measurements are taken using the same routine, where the
program waits for temperature to be within the accepted uncertainty for 1 minute. The
diﬀerence between ﬁne and coarse measurements is that the coarse have steps of 1 K and
accepted uncertainty of 0.05 K, while the ﬁne measurements were done with steps of 0.1
K and uncertainty of 0.025 K.
Tc is estimated using by ﬁnding the two points at which the change in resistance is highest.
Using the temperature of the ﬁrst point the temperature range for the ﬁne measurements
are calculated.
4.3.3
Voltage response
The available drivers for communication with the SR830 lock-in ampliﬁer were found to
be dysfunctional and cause errors, thus the program had to be developed on a lower ab-
straction layer directly handling the commands to the lock-in ampliﬁer. The ﬂow diagram
for the Labview program used to the response measurements is shown in Figure 4.11.
The program is designed to measure the same number of measurements per decade, e.g.
100 measurements betw. 1 and 100 and betw. 100 and 1000. Thus allowing more time
for measurements at a given frequency to ﬁnd the mean value and standard deviation.
==PAGE:36==
5 Measurements and Data processing
30
Figure 4.11: Flow diagram of the Labview program used to measure the voltage response of
the bolometer. The initialize and close routine are shown in Figure H.1.
5
Measurements and Data processing
The following sections will outline the data acquired in the diﬀerent measurements as well
as the data processing performed.
5.1
Susceptibility
The critical temperature can be estimated from a susceptibility measurement. The mea-
surement procedure is described in Section 4.2.4. It has been shown by Yan et al. [51],
that YBCO degrade in the presence of water.
Thus exposure to air can degrade the
superconductivity of the specimens [52]. For this reason a reference sample where the
silver coating was removed was measured 3 months apart to evaluate the changes due to
air exposure in this period. Figure 5.1 shows the measured susceptibility of the reference
sample. The widening of the blue curve is due to the faster rate at which the temperature
is changed. It is seen that the critical temperature is approximately 91 K and after 3
months where the YBCO have been exposed to air it has not changed more than 1 K.
Figures 5.2 and 5.3 shows the corresponding susceptibility measurements of the tapes used
in this work. The tape with the thicker substrate is seen to have a critical temperature
at 91 K, whereas the thinner substrate tape has a slightly lower Tc of 89 K. The code of
the MATLAB program used to process the data is found in Appendix H.2.4 on page 93.
5.2
R-T curve
The measurement of the resistance versus temperature was performed as described in
Section 4.3.2.
A MATLAB program was developed in order to process the data and
the corresponding code is found in Appendix H.2.1 on page 79.
The measured R-T
curves are shown in Figure F.1 along with the corresponding dR

When comparing with the corresponding susceptibility curves for the diﬀerent
substrates seen in Figures 5.2 and 5.3, it becomes evident that the critical temperature
of the tapes is reduced. It is noted that the reduction of Tc is approximately the same
for the samples with the same substrate thickness. Thompson et al. [52], Jin et al. [53]
reported degradation of YBCO when exposed to air due to a reaction with water. An
accelerated degradation of YBCO at 65 ◦C and 90 ◦C is reported by Thompson et al.
[52], Veretnik and Reich [54]. During the fabrication process the sample is heated to 90
◦C after the silver coating has been removed. The reduction of Tc is the assumed to be
caused by the accelerated degradation of YBCO when heated without the protection from
the silver layer [42]. However, optimizing the fabrication process for the highest quality
sensor is outside the scope of this project.

5.3
Voltage response
The voltage response measurements were performed using the setup and program de-
scribed in Sections 4.2 and 4.3.3. MATLAB programs were developed to load and pro-
cesses the data. The ﬁrst program process the raw data, the corresponding code is seen in
Appendix H.2.2 on page 81. The raw data as measured by the lock-in ampliﬁer in phase,
denoted X [V], and 90 ◦out of phase, denoted Y [V]. The amplitude, R [V] and phase θ
[◦] are calculated as:
R =
p
X2 + Y 2,
θ = arctan
 Y
X

.
The mean value and standard deviation for each frequency are calculated and saved
along with the corresponding amplitude and phase. The second program (code in Ap-
pendix H.2.3 on page 85) further processes and plots the data.
As mentioned previously, two diﬀerent chopper blades were used to cover the whole range
of frequencies. The phases from these data series were found not to be aligned when
combined. Figure 5.5 shows the measured phase angle for the sample M3-1018-1 sample
4 long meander, where a third chopper blade overlapping the frequency ranges of the other
two was used to check this misalignment. The corresponding raw data curves for all the
samples are found in Figure F.2(b) on page 67. The curves do not align, even though they
overlap in frequency and were measured using the same setup. None of the three chopper
blades have the same phase angle in the overlapping regions. As the only change in the
system is a diﬀerent chopper blade. The diﬀerence in the measured phase angle with the
diﬀerent blades at the same frequencies is ascribed handling of the phase-locking by the
lock-in ampliﬁer and chopper controller respectively and the misalignment between these
two. To correct for this misalignment the MATLAB program calculates the misalignment
and aligns the higher frequency curves with the lower frequency curves, that corresponds
with the curves found in [44, 48]. Figure 5.7 shows the corrected phase response curves.
6
Discussion
In this section we will ﬁrst consider the thermal bridge setup used in the present work.
We then consider the predictability of the simple RC model by ﬁtting the data. We then
discuss the substrate thickness eﬀect on the responsivity before we compare the produced
detectors with the literature by means of the commonly used ﬁgures of merit.

6.1
Simple thermal bridge analysis
The diﬀerent layers shown in Figure 4.2(a) have diﬀerent heat capacities. Using the values
in Table 6.1 the heat capacity of the yttria buﬀer layer is calculated:
C = cm · ρm · d · A = 1.78
J
kg · K · 3598 kg
m3 · 30 · 10−9 m · 1.8 · 10−6 m2
= 3.5 · 10−10 J
K,
where cm is the mass speciﬁc heat capacity, ρm is the mass density, d is the thickness of the
layer and A is the area of the meander pattern. Similarly, the values for the other buﬀer
layers are calculated to be in the order of 10−10 J
K whereas the heat capacity of YBCO is
in the order of 10−6. The heat capacities of Hastelloy are 0.16 · 10−3 J
K and 0.32 · 10−3 J
K
for the 50 µm and 100 µm substrate thickness respectively. The heat capacities of the
other layers are thus negligible compared to the Hastelloy substrate and the system can
be simpliﬁed by assuming that Ctotal = Csubstrate. Moreover, using the thermal circuit
analogy presented in Section 3.2.3, the resistances in series as shown in Figure 3.7 can be
reduced to a total thermal resistance and the simpler thermal circuit shown in Figure 6.1.
Considering the thermal bridge illustrated in Figure 6.2(a). The incoming heat ﬂux q hits
the sensor in the center and the generated heat is transported to each side and dumped
to the coldhead. The symmetry in the system allows us to consider half the heat being
absorbed and transported to the coldhead in one side. Thus we can consider the system
marked with the dashed square in the ﬁgure. If we ﬁrst consider the simple case without
the superconductor, where q hit the substrate directly. Assuming that half the total laser
power is absorbed in the substrate in one end and all the heat is transported to the other
end, as indicated in Figure 6.2(b). The temperature diﬀerence across the thick substrate

Thus the ∆T through the contact area is on the same order of magnitude as the heat

and the coldhead greatly reduces the eﬀect of the contact resistance. Had the contact

Compared to the corresponding temperature diﬀerence through the substrate of 1.7 ·
10−4 K.
The eﬀect of the cold head boundary would have completely dominated the
response without the thermal bridge. The thermal conductance through the system is
thus dominated by the conductance through the substrate and the substrate-coldhead
boundary.
The thermal boundary resistance in the contact between the substrate and the coldhead
normally dominates the thermal conductivity of the bolometer. However, as we saw in
this section, introducing the thermal bridge together with a relatively large contact area
between the substrate and cold head, changes the eﬀect of the heat transfer through the
substrate to be on the same order of magnitude as the contact resistance. In fact the
predicted temperature diﬀerence across the thermal bridge is slightly higher than over
the contact. In the next section we will take a look at how well the simple RC model
predict the response of this system.

thermal conductance becomes frequency dependent [67] which the simple model do not
account for. It is seen that the ﬁt of the voltage response curves generally ﬁt better than
the phase response and the resulting ﬁtted parameters have less uncertainty. It is thus
concluded that the simple model can give a good prediction of the responsivity especially
at lower frequencies. For a more precise prediction of the responsivity more sophisticated

==PAGE:47==

response of the 50 µm substrate samples. As both curves of thick substrate have a higher

The resistance Re = ρe · l
A is dependent on the electric resistivity, ρe, the length l and the
cross sectional area, A, of the resistor. The longer meander line will increase the length
and thus the resistance. This is seen to be the case for the thin substrate. On the other
hand, the thicker substrate is seen to have a lower response for the longer meander line.
The wirebonds on the original Al-wirebonded 100 µm long meander sample broke and
the measurements had to be done using the replacement sample from the batch to be Ag
wirebonded, which failed and was later Al wirebonded instead. The extra heatup for the
failed Ag wirebond may have caused an accelerated degradation of the YBCO, as described
previously. The degraded superconductor will have a higher electrical resistance closer
to the normal conducting state. The corresponding lower dR
dT result in a lower response.

the substrate thickness have an eﬀect on the response of the bolometers.

Fardmanesh et al. [69] reports that thinning the substrate provides and increase in detec-
tivity for single crystalline samples with < 0.23 µm YBCO ﬁlm on 50 µm thick substrates
of SrTiO3, MgO and LaAlO3. However, they also report that there is an optimum point
beyond which thinning the substrate lowers the detectivity. The optimum substrate thick-
ness of the substrate depends on thermal parameters and modulation frequency. In the
present system the superconducting layer is an order of magnitude larger, buﬀer layers
are introduced and a thermal bridge is used. The optimal thickness for substrate in this
setup could possibly be larger and the better detectivity and responsivity of the thick
substrate, could thus be a result of thickness being closer to the optimal value for the
present system. However, ﬁnding the optimal thickness of the Hastelloy substrate is a
subject for future project.

Compared with the theoretical value above the measured NEP is 1-2 orders of magnitude
from the theoretical max value. The measurements are thus not limited by the noise of
bolometer but rather excess noise in the measurement setup. Up to 2 orders of magnitude
improvement of the NEP from 8 · 10−6 W · Hz−1/2 to the theoretical NEP = 4.5 · 10−9 W ·
Hz−1/2 should be possible by reducing the excess noise in the measurement setup. The
corresponding detectivity will be 2 orders of magnitude larger and the bolometers will
begin to be comparable to the state-of-the-art sensors shown in Figure 6.7.

7
Conclusion
Bolometer detectors were produced on two Coated Conductors (CCs) from SuperPower
Inc. with diﬀerent substrate thicknesses using UV-lithography. Four diﬀerent detectors
were produced having a long and short meander line pattern on both the CCs.
A measurement setup and automatic data acquisition software were developed. The lab
power supply was found to provide a very noisy current output and a new current supply
==PAGE:52==
8 Outlook
46
circuit was developed to provide a constant current with far lower noise. In order to
measure the eﬀect of the substrate thickness a setup with a thermal bridge was designed,
as the thermal contact resistance between the substrate and the coldhead would otherwise
have dominated the thermal conductance through the bolometer.
The critical temperature, Tc, of the superconductor was found to be decreased during
the fabrication process. The accelerated degradation of heated YBa2Cu3O7−x (YBCO)
exposed to air, as reported in the literature, is suspected to be the cause of the degradation.
The measured response of the bolometer was ﬁtted using the simple RC model:
rv =
I · dRe
dT
Ge · (1 + i · ωs · τe)
where f = ωs·(2π)−1 is the modulation frequency, Ge is the eﬀective thermal conductance
and τe =
C
Ge is the corresponding eﬀective time response. The ﬁt was found to provide
a good prediction of the response of the bolometer, especially at lower frequencies. At
higher frequencies the thermal diﬀusion length, L, becomes comparable to the thickness
of the substrate. As a result the thermal conductivity becomes frequency dependent.
A diﬀerence between the response of the thin and thick substrate was seen. However, one
of the detectors with the thick substrate was found to have a lower responsivity, possibly
due to an extra heat-up during fabrication and a corresponding accelerated degradation
of the YBCO superconducting layer as reported in the literature.
The measurements were found to have excess noise from the measurement setup increas-
ing the The Noise Equivalent Power (NEP) several orders of magnitudes above the the-
oretically estimated value.
The measured NEP was found to be between 3 · 10−7 to
8 · 10−6 W · Hz−1/2, whereas the theoretically estimated value lies between 4.5 · 10−9 to
1.4 · 10−8 W · Hz−1/2. The detectivity of the devices was found to have values from 105 to
5 · 105 cm ·
√
Hz · W−1. The detectivity was an order of magnitude lower than reported
in the literature. However, the detectivity can be greatly improved by lowering the NEP
through reducing the excess noise in the measurement system. The impulse response of
8·10−5 cm· J−1 was found to be comparable to other detectors reported in the literature.
It can thus be concluded that a coated conductor is a feasible template for production of a
bolometric sensor. Furthermore, it has been documented that the substrate thickness have
an eﬀect on the responsivity of the bolometer. Finally, the simple RC model was found to
give a good prediction of the responsivity of the bolometer. Though at high frequencies
the model have to be extended to take frequency dependent eﬀects into account.
8
Outlook
This section present recommendations for future experiments.
The thermal bridge was found to change the knee frequency and the corresponding thermal
diﬀusion length of the substrate. An optimum length of the thermal bridge seems to be
indicated by the diﬀusion length and is subject to further studies. The eﬀect of the thermal
bridge could possibly prove to be a way of tweaking the response of the bolometer.
A discrepancy between the measured data and the simple RC model was seen at high
==PAGE:53==
8 Outlook
47
frequencies. The high frequency behavior of the coated conductors should be investigated
further and compared to the single crystalline detectors.
The measurement setup was found to have and excess amount of noise. Thus limiting the
detectivity of the produced bolometers. The Noise Equivalent Power of the CC bolometers
themselves thus still need to be measured and the estimated improvement in NEP of 2
orders of magnitude by reducing the excess noise is to be investigated further.

Terms and abbreviations
48
List of Terms
Isothermal nodes A discrete region, which is small enough to assume that the temper-
ature gradient is zero.
Each node is characterized by heat capacity, temperature, heat dissipation as well
as conductive and radiative interfaces with the surrounding nodes. 17
Quench When a quench occurs part of the superconductor enters the normal conducting
state. Can be caused by ﬂux jumps with large energy dissipation, as a result of
strong external disturbances, such as magnetic, thermal or mechanical nature. 20

Appendix A
Article draft
A.1
Abstract
A.2
Introduction
A.3
Experimental
A.3.1
Device fabrication
The bolometric pattern is etched on two commercially available Coated Conductors (CC) from
SuperPower Inc. The CCs have substrates of 50 µm and 100 µm respectively. As described by
[41] there are ﬁve buﬀer layers between the substrate and the HTS and a silvercoating is covering
both sides of the CC. The patterning is performed with UV lithography and the mask designed
in [44] using the resist AZ5214E and developer AZ351B in a 1:5 water solution. The mask is
removed using Acetone. Firstly, using a negative photo resist the silver coating is etched away
using a solution of 10 % NH3OH, 10 % H2O2 and 80 % H2O, while leaving silver on the four
pads contact pads, where the electrical contacts are Al wire bonded. The bolometer pattern is
transferred to the CC using a positive resist and the pattern is etched in the superconductor and
buﬀer layers using phosphoric acid in a water solution of 1:100. Figure A.1(a) shows an optical
dark-ﬁeld image of the produced bolometer chip. The samples are glued to the back of a PCB
and wire bonded with Al as seen on Figure A.1(b).
(a)
(b)
Figure A.1: (a) an optical darkﬁeld image of the produced bolometer chip on the ﬁrst sample
produced. It is seen that the produced pattern is clearly visible with no apparent defects.
(b) the sample holder with the sample mounted.
A.3.2
Measurements
A measurement setup was assembled consisting of a Microstat HE2 continous ﬂow cryostat from
Oxford instruments, a chopper New Focus model 3502 with chopper blades for the frequency
range from 4 Hz to 10.65 kHz, a red 635 nm diode laser from Thorlabs with an intensity of 2.296
mW ±0.005 mW, a lock-in ampliﬁer SR830 from Stanford Research where the internal oscillator
is used do modulate the chopper and Agilent 34401A multimeters for measuring resistance and
DC voltage/current. A current biasing circuit powered by a Agilent E3631A power supply, was
developed for a stable supply current. The circuit consist of 2N6292G transistor regulated by a
==PAGE:64==

LM741 OPAMP and a 7812 voltage regulator with pontentiometer and resistances to provide a
current of 0.68 mA.

Appendix D
Theory
D.1
Superconductivity
Introduction
In 1933 Meissner and Ochsenfeld [25] showed that magnetic ﬂux is expelled from the interior of
the superconductor, as described in Section 3.1.2. There is a limitation on how large a ﬁeld that
==PAGE:66==
D.1
Superconductivity
60
can be expelled. This leads to the third critical parameter called the critical ﬁeld, Hc. This is
described in Sections 3.1.2 and 3.1.3. Two types of superconductors behave diﬀerently at Hc. The
two types are described in Appendix D.1.3.
D.1.1
Meissner eﬀect
When the superconductor is in the superconducting state screening currents arise on the surface,
thus inducing a ﬁeld that cancels the applied ﬁeld inside the superconductor. The ﬁeld lines from
the induced magnetization exit the superconductor at the poles in the direction opposite to the
applied ﬁeld hereby lowering the ﬁeld outside the superconductor, while strengthening the ﬁeld at
equator where the directions of the two ﬁelds are the same. The superconductor is then said to
be in the Meissner state and correspondingly the eﬀect is called the Meissner eﬀect. The Meissner
eﬀect is described mathematically by the London equation, see Equation (D.2).
D.1.2
London equation and penetration depth
The phenomenon leading to superconductivity is the phonon-mediated pairing of electrons in
the so called Cooper pairs, as ﬁrst described for an isolated pair’s formation in a metal by
Cooper [70] in 1956. The many electron pairs forming were described the year after in the full
Bardeen–Cooper–Schrieﬀer (BCS) theory by J. Bardeen, L. Cooper and J. Schrieﬀer [71, 72].
The superconducting electrons are forming the superconducting condensate and the concentra-
tion of the Cooper paired electrons is denoted ns.
These electrons can be regarded as a Bo-
son gas.
Denoting the particle probability amplitude by ψ (⃗r) and assume the concentration
ns = ψψ∗= constant. At absolute zero this results in [73]:
ψ = n

2 · ei·θ(⃗r) ;

of a particle [73]:

D.1.3
Type I and II superconductors
Another important length scale known as the coherence length, ξ, arises from the Ginsburg Landau
Theory (see [24]):
ξ (T) =


where ℏ= 1.05459 · 10−34 [ J · s] is the reduced Planck constant, m is the electron mass and
α is a parameter resulting from a temperature dependent axpansion.
ξ expresses the length
over which the concentration of superconducting electrons ns can change, see Appendix D.1.4

Type I
As described in Section 3.1.2 the superconductor is completely shielded in the Meissner
state. Figure D.1(a) sketches the magnetization as a function of applied ﬁeld. It is seen that the
slope of the curve corresponds to χ = −1. When the well-deﬁned critical ﬁeld Hc is reached the
superconductor switches abruptly from the Meissner state to the normal state, thus having full
penetration of magnetic ﬂux [24].
Type II
The second type behaves like the ﬁrst in the Meissner state, completely shielding the
interior of the superconductor from the applied magnetic ﬁeld. However, when the applied ﬁeld
reaches the critical ﬁeld Hc1 the magnetic ﬁeld starts to penetrate the Type II superconductor, as
shown on Figure D.1(b). This state is called the mixed state, where the magnetic ﬂux penetrates
the superconductor in the form of ﬂux lines, as described in the following section.

Figure D.1: Sketches the behavior of magnetization in type I and II superconductors in an
increasing applied ﬁeld. The behavior of type I is shown in (a) while type II is sketched in
(b). Both completely shield the external ﬁeld in the Meissner state. While type I abruptly
switches to complete ﬂux penetration at the critical ﬁeld Hc, type II is gradually penetrated
when the applied ﬁeld is increased beyond the critical ﬁeld Hc1 until full penetration is
reached at Hc2. Figure reproduced after [74].
D.1.4
Pinning
As mentioned in the previous section, Type II superconductors are gradually penetrated by mag-
netic ﬂux when the applied ﬁeld is increased beyond the critical ﬁeld Hc1. This happens in normal
conducting areas, where the density of superconducting electrons ns = 0, surrounded by vortices
of quantized circulation of supercurrents [24]. The two length scales λL and ξ given by Equa-
tions (D.1) and (D.4) are illustrated in Figure D.2(a). The vortices can be trapped in regions
where ns is lower due to defects or impurities in the superconductor, as the energy required to
create the normal conducting vortex core is lowered. This process is called pinning and the optimal
size for a pinning site must be comparable to the coherence length ξ [75].
2
λ
ns
defect
B(x)
(a)
(b)
Figure D.2:
(a) Sketch of the density of superconducting electrons ns across a type II
superconductor penetrated by a magnetic ﬁeld B(x). The “hole” with ns = 0 is a vortex
line. Placing the vortex where ns is already lowered lowers the energy [75, 74]. (b) Sketch
of the deﬁnition of Ic or Jc for transport measurement. The plot can be read as either a
J-E or I-V curve. Curve has a linear V vs. I behavior at low and high values of I, while
the intermediate region is highly non-linear. Figure after [24]
==PAGE:69==
E Experimental
63
D.1.5
Transport current measurement
Figure D.2(b) shows a sketch of a typical transport current measurement, where the voltage V
versus current I is measured in the presence of an external magnetic ﬁeld, which has created a
vortex density in the superconductor. This gives rise to three regions, two of which are linear:
Thermally Assisted Flux Flow (TAFF) in the low current region and ﬂux ﬂow in the high current
region.
The non-linear region in-between is called ﬂux creep.
The latter two are common in
low-Tc research, but with the higher temperatures of high-Tc the TAFF is more evident [24]. In
the TAFF and ﬂux creep regions thermal forces may result in the escape of a vortex from the
potential minimum at a pinning site, thus creating a movement of the vortices [24]. In the ﬂux
ﬂow region the Lorentz force exceeds the pinning force and a resulting movement of the vortices
is found. These gives rise to a resistance from the viscous drag of the surrounding medium [24].
For an in-depth treatment of the diﬀerent regions the reader is referred to Fossheim and Sudbø
[24].
One way to deﬁne Ic is in the region crossing over from TAFF to ﬂux ﬂow at the point where
E = 1 µ V
cm [24], as marked on Figure D.2(b). All three critical parameters impose limitations on
the superconducting state and deﬁne the critical surface [75] as sketched in Figure 3.2.
D.1.6
Powder in Tubes
The ﬁrst generation of HTS wires were the so-called Powder-in-tube (PIT) consisting of metal-
oxide ﬁlled silver tubes [24].
One example of PIT is with Bi2Sr2Ca2Cu3Ox (Bi-2223) powder
loaded into the tubes, which are then extruded, drawn/rolled and heat treated in an atmosphere
to form superconducting Bi-2223 tapes [24].
The same has been attempted with YBCO [76].
However, the Jc is limited to ∼3300
A
cm2 due to the decoupling of the superconducting CuO2
planes at grain boundaries [39].

E.3.1
Cryogenic system
Internal wiring
Due to the excellent resistance to chemical attacks and mechanical abrasion
[46], the coating of the wire SH Therm 210 Grd.2 from SH Elektrodraht proved diﬃcult to remove.
The coating consists of 7-12 layers of polyesterimide as base layer and 6-8 layers of polyamideimide
as top layer [77]. Diﬀerent approaches were tried to remove the coating: KOH and NaOH etch,
as well as Xylene was tried without a result. The strong Organic solvents Dimethyl sulfoxide,
DMSO and Dimethylformamide, DMF were also ineﬀective. Finally, as described in patent [78]
the coating could be removed in NaOH heating to 100 ◦C, though the etch took 2 hours rather
than the 10 minutes described in the patent.

(G.1)
Temperatures Ti,0 are replaced by the newly calculated temperatures Ti and the calculation is
repeated until the diﬀerence between the two temperatures are small enough that a steady state
condition can be assumed. Even though δTi,0 might not be suﬃciently small initially, this criterion
will be fulﬁlled for the ﬁnal iterations and hereby the calculated temperatures will be correct [37].
Considering the system sketched in Figure 3.7, where all the nodes are only in contact with two
other nodes. Now deﬁning:

Ti · ai,i −Ti+1 · ai,i+1 −Ti−1 · ai,i−1 = bi(t).
(G.5)
The ﬁnal layer (n) in contact with the heat sink (layer n+1), which have have the constant
temperature T0, Equation (G.5) becomes:
Tn · an,n −Tn−1 · an,n−1 = bi(t) + T0 · an,n+1.

G.1.3

For a given modulation frequency of the irradiation on the bolometer the resulting temperature
of the superconducting node can be calculated using Equation (G.7). Once the ﬁnal temperature
of the current biased superconducting node is found the voltage response can be calculated:
Usc = Isc · dResc

· (Tsc −T0) ,

The simulation is implemented in a MATLAB program where Equation (G.15) is solved to ﬁnd
the temperatures for a given time. The newly calculated temperatures are then used to calculate
new matrices A and b at a time step later and the new temperatures are estimated. The initial
temperatures for the transient heat calculations are calculated using the steady state model given
by Equation (G.1) with the initial temperatures being the temperature of the cold ﬁnger. The
code for the MATLAB program is found in Appendix H.2.5 on page 94 and the material constants
for the diﬀerent layers in the model is given in Table 6.1.
G.3
Alternative models
A more sophisticated model is reported by Mohajeri et al. [44], where the raise in temperature is
given by:
∆T = q
Zb + Rfb
1 + iωZbCf + iωRfbCf
,
==PAGE:78==
G.3
Alternative models
72
where q
 W
m2

is the heat ﬂux from radiation absorption, Rfb is the boundary resistance between
YBCO and the buﬀer layer. The equivalent thermal impedance Zb is given by [44]:
Zb =
Zs + Rbs
1 + iωZsCb + iωRbsCb
,
where Rbs is the boundary resistance between the buﬀer layer and the substrate and Cb is the
heat capacity of the buﬀer layer. The equivalent thermal impedance of the substrate Zs is given
by [44]:
Zs = exp(γL) + Γ exp(−γL)
exp(γL) −Γ exp(−γL) ·
1
iωCsks
,
where ks and Cs are the thermal conductivity and heat capacity of the substrate. The character-
istic thermal impedance of the substrate material γ is given by [79]:
γ = 1 + i
√
2 ·
rωcs
ks
,
Γ =
Rcs −
1
iωcsks
Rcs +
1
iωcsks
,
where Rcs is the coldhead-substrate boundary resistance. As seen on Figures G.3 and G.4 this
model ﬁts the data better than the simple RC model. An improvement is suggested by Fardmanesh
et al. [48] is adding a frequency dependence to Cs as:
Cs(f) = b · A ·
s
Cs,const.ks
πf
,
where A is the area of the sensor and b is a ﬁtting parameter compensating for the drop of
amplitude. The eﬀect of the parameter by can be seen in Figure G.2 on page 74 and a optimal
value is estimated to b = −105. The improvement of the model with the frequency dependence of
Cs is seen on Figures G.3 and G.4. Though the prediction of the data has improved it is still not
suﬃciently predicting the response of the device. Without detailed knowledge about the ﬁtting
parameters of the given model it has not been possible to reproduce the model results reported
by Mohajeri et al. [44].
The model is shown in Figures G.3 and G.4 along data from Mohajeri et al. [44]. The simple model
is found to be insuﬃcient to describe the more sophisticated system involving two layers as is the
case with the data set without buﬀer layer shown in the ﬁgure. There are several ways to modify
the simple RC model. One such is adding a frequency dependence to the thermal conductance as
suggested by Hu and Richards [67]. However, as reported by Fardmanesh [79] the modiﬁcation is
still not suﬃcient to ﬁt the data.
